<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/θ/: &#39;th&#39; in &#39;thigh&#39;">θ</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">BIZ</span>-məth</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_bismuth" title="Template:Infobox bismuth"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_bismuth" title="Template talk:Infobox bismuth"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_bismuth&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Bismuth</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>Bi</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 83. It is a <a href="/wiki/Pentavalent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentavalent">pentavalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metal</a> and one of the <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogens</a> with chemical properties resembling its lighter homologs <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>. Elemental bismuth may occur naturally, although its <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> form important commercial ores. The <a href="/wiki/Free_element" title="Free element">free element</a> is 86% as dense as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. It is a brittle metal with a silvery white color when freshly produced, but surface <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation</a> can give it a pink tinge. Bismuth is marginally <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>, and the most naturally <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetic</a> element, and has one of the lowest values of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> among metals.
</p><p>Bismuth metal has been known since ancient times, although it was often confused with lead and tin, which share some physical properties. The etymology is uncertain, but possibly comes from Arabic <i>bi ismid</i>, meaning having the properties of antimony<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> or the German words <i>weiße Masse</i> or <i>Wismuth</i> ("white mass"), translated in the mid-sixteenth century to <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> <i>bisemutum</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-oet_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oet-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth was long considered the element with the highest atomic mass that is stable, but in 2003 it was discovered to be extremely weakly <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>: its only <a href="/wiki/Primordial_isotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Primordial isotope">primordial isotope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">bismuth-209</a>, decays via <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> more than a <a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> times the estimated <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Because of its tremendously long half-life, bismuth may still be considered stable for almost all purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth compounds account for about half the production of bismuth. They are used in cosmetics, pigments, and a few pharmaceuticals, notably <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">bismuth subsalicylate</a>, used to treat diarrhea.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth's unusual propensity to expand upon freezing is responsible for some of its uses, such as in casting of printing type.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth has unusually low <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> for a heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> As the toxicity of lead has become more apparent in recent years, there is an increasing use of bismuth alloys (presently about a third of bismuth production) as a replacement for lead.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png/110px-Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png" width="110" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png/165px-Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png/220px-Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuth_symbol_by_Torbern_Bergman.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alchemical_symbol" title="Alchemical symbol">Alchemical symbol</a> used by <a href="/wiki/Torbern_Bergman" title="Torbern Bergman">Torbern Bergman</a>, 1775</div></div></div>
<p>The name <i>bismuth</i> dates from around the 1660s, and is of uncertain etymology. It is one of the first 10 metals to have been discovered. Bismuth appears in the 1660s, from obsolete <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Bismuth</i></i>, <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Wismut</i></i>, <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Wissmuth</i></i> (early 16th century); perhaps related to <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i><i lang="goh" title="Old High German language text">hwiz</i></i> ("white").<sup id="cite_ref-oet_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oet-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">bisemutum</i></i> (due to <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Georgius Agricola</a>, who Latinized many German mining and technical words) is from the German <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Wismuth</i></i>, perhaps from <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">weiße Masse</i></i>, "white mass".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The element was confused in early times with <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and lead because of its resemblance to those elements. Bismuth has been known since ancient times, so no one person is credited with its discovery. <a href="/wiki/Georgius_Agricola" title="Georgius Agricola">Agricola</a>, in <a href="/wiki/De_Natura_Fossilium" title="De Natura Fossilium">De Natura Fossilium</a> (c. 1546) states that bismuth is a distinct metal in a family of metals including tin and lead. This was based on observation of the metals and their physical properties.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Miners in the age of alchemy also gave bismuth the name <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">tectum argenti</i>,</i> or "silver being made," in the sense of silver still in the process of being formed within the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning with <a href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_Heinrich_Pott&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Johann Heinrich Pott (page does not exist)">Johann Heinrich Pott</a> in 1738,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torbern_Olof_Bergman" class="mw-redirect" title="Torbern Olof Bergman">Torbern Olof Bergman</a>, the distinctness of lead and bismuth became clear, and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Fran%C3%A7ois_Geoffroy" title="Claude François Geoffroy">Claude François Geoffroy</a> demonstrated in 1753 that this metal is distinct from lead and tin.<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
Bismuth was also known to the <a href="/wiki/Incas" class="mw-redirect" title="Incas">Incas</a> and used (along with the usual copper and tin) in a special <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_bronze" title="Bismuth bronze">bronze alloy</a> for knives.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bi-crystal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bi-crystal.jpg/170px-Bi-crystal.jpg" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bi-crystal.jpg/255px-Bi-crystal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Bi-crystal.jpg/340px-Bi-crystal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2492" data-file-height="3276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bi-crystal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bismuth crystal illustrating the many iridescent refraction hues of its oxide surface</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg/220px-Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg/330px-Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg/440px-Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5176" data-file-height="3519" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wismut_Kristall_und_1cm3_Wuerfel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artificially grown bismuth crystal illustrating the stairstep crystal structure, with a 1&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> cube of bismuth metal</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bismuth is a brittle metal with a white, silver-pink hue, often with an <a href="/wiki/Iridescence" title="Iridescence">iridescent</a> <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_oxide" title="Bismuth(III) oxide">oxide</a> tarnish showing many colors from yellow to blue. The spiral, stair-stepped structure of bismuth crystals is the result of a higher growth rate around the outside edges than on the inside edges. The variations in the thickness of the oxide layer that forms on the surface of the crystal cause different wavelengths of light to interfere upon reflection, thus displaying a rainbow of colors. When <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">burned</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, bismuth burns with a blue <a href="/wiki/Flame" title="Flame">flame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth oxide">its oxide</a> forms yellow <a href="/wiki/Vapor" title="Vapor">fumes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> is much lower than that of its neighbors in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, such as lead, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>.
</p><p>
No other metal is verified to be more naturally <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetic</a> than bismuth.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Superdiamagnetism" title="Superdiamagnetism">Superdiamagnetism</a> is a different physical phenomenon.) Of any metal, it has one of the lowest values of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> (after <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, and maybe <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>) and the highest <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">Hall coefficient</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> It has a high <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">electrical resistivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> When deposited in sufficiently thin layers on a substrate, bismuth is a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a>, despite being a <a href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg/220px-Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg/330px-Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg/440px-Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuth_sample_1B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bismuth sample demonstrating staircase structure and iridescent properties</div></div></div><p>Elemental bismuth is <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">denser</a> in the liquid phase than the solid, a characteristic it shares with <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-w768_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w768-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth expands 3.32% on solidification; therefore, it was long a component of low-melting <a href="/wiki/Typesetting" title="Typesetting">typesetting</a> <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>, where it compensated for the contraction of the other alloying components<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> to form almost isostatic <a href="/wiki/Lead-bismuth_eutectic" title="Lead-bismuth eutectic">bismuth-lead eutectic</a> alloys.
</p><p>Though virtually unseen in nature, high-purity bismuth can form distinctive, colorful <a href="/wiki/Hopper_crystal" title="Hopper crystal">hopper crystals</a>. It is relatively nontoxic and has a low melting point just above 271&#160;°C, so crystals may be grown using a household stove, although the resulting crystals will tend to be lower quality than lab-grown crystals.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At ambient conditions bismuth shares the same layered structure as the metallic forms of <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-w767_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w767-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> crystallizing in the <a href="/wiki/Trigonal_crystal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonal crystal system">rhombohedral lattice</a><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Pearson_symbol" title="Pearson symbol">Pearson symbol</a> hR6, <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> R<span style="text-decoration:overline;">3</span>m No. 166), which is often classed into trigonal or hexagonal crystal systems.<sup id="cite_ref-str_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-str-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> When compressed at room temperature, this Bi-I structure changes first to the <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monoclinic">monoclinic</a> Bi-II at 2.55 GPa, then to the <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetragonal">tetragonal</a> Bi-III at 2.7 GPa, and finally to the <a href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" class="mw-redirect" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a> Bi-IV at 7.7 GPa. The corresponding transitions can be monitored via changes in electrical conductivity; they are rather reproducible and abrupt, and are therefore used for calibration of high-pressure equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_characteristics">Chemical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bismuth is stable to both dry and moist air at ordinary temperatures. When red-hot, it reacts with water to make bismuth(III) oxide.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Bi + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>It reacts with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(V)_fluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth(V) fluoride">bismuth(V) fluoride</a> at 500&#160;°C or <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_fluoride" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth(III) fluoride">bismuth(III) fluoride</a> at lower temperatures (typically from Bi melts); with other <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> it yields only bismuth(III) halides.<sup id="cite_ref-w770_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w770-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-g559_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g559-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-k185_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k185-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The trihalides are corrosive and easily react with moisture, forming <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oxyhalides&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Oxyhalides (page does not exist)">oxyhalides</a> with the formula BiOX.<sup id="cite_ref-s9_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s9-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Bi + 3 X<sub>2</sub> → 2 BiX<sub>3</sub> (X = F, Cl, Br, I)</dd></dl>
<p>Bismuth dissolves in concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> to make <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth(III)_sulfate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bismuth(III) sulfate (page does not exist)">bismuth(III) sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>6 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> + 2 Bi → 6 H<sub>2</sub>O + Bi<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 3 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>It reacts with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_nitrate" title="Bismuth(III) nitrate">bismuth(III) nitrate</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd>Bi + 6 HNO<sub>3</sub> → 3 H<sub>2</sub>O + 3 NO<sub>2</sub> + Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>It also dissolves in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, but only with oxygen present.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 Bi + 3 O<sub>2</sub> + 12 HCl → 4 BiCl<sub>3</sub> + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>It is used as a <a href="/wiki/Transmetalation" title="Transmetalation">transmetalating</a> agent in the synthesis of alkaline-earth metal complexes:
</p>
<dl><dd>3 Ba + 2 BiPh<sub>3</sub> → 3 BaPh<sub>2</sub> + 2 Bi</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_bismuth" title="Isotopes of bismuth">Isotopes of bismuth</a></div>
<p>The only primordial <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of bismuth, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">bismuth-209</a>, was traditionally regarded as the heaviest stable isotope, but it had long been suspected<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> to be unstable on theoretical grounds. This was finally demonstrated in 2003, when researchers at the Institut d'Astrophysique Spatiale in <a href="/wiki/Orsay" title="Orsay">Orsay</a>, France, measured the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha emission</a> <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">209</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> to be <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7026599594400000000♠</span>1.9<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>19</sup>&#160;years</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> over a <a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> times longer than the current estimated <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Owing to its extraordinarily long half-life, for all presently known medical and industrial applications, bismuth can be treated as if it is stable and nonradioactive. The radioactivity is of academic interest because bismuth is one of a few elements whose radioactivity was suspected and theoretically predicted before being detected in the laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth has the longest known alpha decay half-life, although <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_tellurium" title="Isotopes of tellurium">tellurium-128</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a> half-life of over <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7031694267200000000♠</span>2.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>24</sup>&#160;years</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-NUBASE_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth's extremely long half life means that less than one billionth of the bismuth present at the formation of the planet Earth would have decayed into thallium since then.
</p><p>Several isotopes of bismuth with short half-lives occur within the radioactive disintegration chains of <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, and more have been synthesized experimentally. Bismuth-213 is also found on the decay chain of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">uranium-233</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercially, the radioactive isotope bismuth-213 can be produced by bombarding <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a> photons from a <a href="/wiki/Linear_particle_accelerator" title="Linear particle accelerator">linear particle accelerator</a>. In 1997, an antibody conjugate with bismuth-213, which has a 45-minute half-life and decays with the emission of an alpha particle, was used to treat patients with leukemia. This isotope has also been tried in cancer treatment, for example, in the targeted alpha therapy (TAT) program.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_compounds">Chemical compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chemical compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Bismuth_compounds" title="Category:Bismuth compounds">Category:Bismuth compounds</a>.</div>
<p>Bismuth forms trivalent and pentavalent compounds, the trivalent ones being more common. Many of its chemical properties are similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, although they are less toxic than derivatives of those lighter elements.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_sulfides">Oxides and sulfides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Oxides and sulfides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At elevated temperatures, the vapors of the metal combine rapidly with oxygen, forming the yellow trioxide, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_oxide" title="Bismuth(III) oxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-w768_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w768-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-g553_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g553-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> When molten, at temperatures above 710&#160;°C, this oxide corrodes any metal oxide, and even platinum.<sup id="cite_ref-k185_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k185-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> On reaction with base, it forms two series of <a href="/wiki/Oxyanion" title="Oxyanion">oxyanions</a>: <span class="chemf nowrap">BiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, which is polymeric and forms linear chains, and <span class="chemf nowrap">BiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>. The anion in <span class="chemf nowrap">Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>BiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> is actually a cubic octameric anion, <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sub></span></span>, whereas the anion in <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>BiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> is tetrameric.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The dark red bismuth(V) oxide, <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, is unstable, liberating <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen"><span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> gas upon heating.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The compound <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bismuthate" title="Sodium bismuthate">NaBiO<sub>3</sub></a> is a strong oxidising agent.<sup id="cite_ref-g578_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g578-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth sulfide, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_sulfide" title="Bismuth(III) sulfide"><span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>, occurs naturally in bismuth ores.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> It is also produced by the combination of molten bismuth and sulfur.<sup id="cite_ref-g559_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g559-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MatlockiteStructure.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/MatlockiteStructure.png/220px-MatlockiteStructure.png" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/MatlockiteStructure.png/330px-MatlockiteStructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/MatlockiteStructure.png/440px-MatlockiteStructure.png 2x" data-file-width="1047" data-file-height="904" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MatlockiteStructure.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bismuth oxychloride (BiOCl) structure (mineral <a href="/wiki/Bismoclite" title="Bismoclite">bismoclite</a>). Bismuth atoms shown as grey, oxygen red, chlorine green.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxychloride" title="Bismuth oxychloride">Bismuth oxychloride</a> (BiOCl, see figure at right) and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxynitrate" title="Bismuth oxynitrate">bismuth oxynitrate</a> (BiONO<sub>3</sub>) stoichiometrically appear as simple anionic salts of the bismuthyl(III) cation (BiO<sup>+</sup>) which commonly occurs in aqueous bismuth compounds. However, in the case of BiOCl, the salt crystal forms in a structure of alternating plates of Bi, O, and Cl atoms, with each oxygen coordinating with four bismuth atoms in the adjacent plane. This mineral compound is used as a pigment and cosmetic (see below).<sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bismuthine_and_bismuthides">Bismuthine and bismuthides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bismuthine and bismuthides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Unlike the lighter <a href="/wiki/Pnictogen" title="Pnictogen">pnictogens</a> nitrogen, phosphorus, and arsenic, but similar to <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, bismuth does not form a stable <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a>. Bismuth hydride, <a href="/wiki/Bismuthine" title="Bismuthine">bismuthine</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">BiH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), is an <a href="/wiki/Endothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> compound that spontaneously decomposes at room temperature. It is stable only below −60&#160;°C.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Bismuthides are <a href="/wiki/Intermetallic" title="Intermetallic">intermetallic</a> compounds between bismuth and other metals.
</p><p>In 2014 researchers discovered that sodium bismuthide can exist as a form of matter called a “three-dimensional topological Dirac semi-metal” (3DTDS) that possess 3D <a href="/wiki/Dirac_fermion" title="Dirac fermion">Dirac fermions</a> in bulk. It is a natural, three-dimensional counterpart to <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> with similar <a href="/wiki/Electron_mobility" title="Electron mobility">electron mobility</a> and velocity. Graphene and <a href="/wiki/Topological_insulator" title="Topological insulator">topological insulators</a> (such as those in 3DTDS) are both crystalline materials that are electrically insulating inside but conducting on the surface, allowing them to function as <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a> and other electronic devices. While sodium bismuthide (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Bi</span>) is too unstable to be used in devices without packaging, it can demonstrate potential applications of 3DTDS systems, which offer distinct efficiency and fabrication advantages over planar graphene in <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">spintronics</a> applications.
<sup id="cite_ref-k1401_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k1401-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a> of bismuth in low oxidation states have been shown to adopt unusual structures. What was originally thought to be bismuth(I) chloride, BiCl, turns out to be a complex compound consisting of Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span> cations and BiCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span> and Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span> anions.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gillespie1_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillespie1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span> cation has a distorted tricapped <a href="/wiki/Trigonal_prism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonal prism">trigonal prismatic</a> molecular geometry, and is also found in <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sub></span>Hf<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sub></span></span>, which is prepared by reducing a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hafnium(IV)_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Hafnium(IV) chloride">hafnium(IV) chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_chloride" title="Bismuth chloride">bismuth chloride</a> with elemental bismuth, having the structure <span class="chemf nowrap">[Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>] [Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span>] [HfCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>50</span></sup> Other polyatomic bismuth cations are also known, such as Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span>, found in <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span>(AlCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-gillespie1_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gillespie1-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth also forms a low-valence bromide with the same structure as "BiCl". There is a <i>true</i> monoiodide, BiI, which contains chains of <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> units. BiI decomposes upon heating to the triiodide, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_iodide" title="Bismuth(III) iodide"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>, and elemental bismuth. A monobromide of the same structure also exists.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
In oxidation state +3, bismuth forms trihalides with all of the halogens: <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_trifluoride" title="Bismuth trifluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth(III) chloride"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_tribromide" title="Bismuth tribromide"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiBr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_iodide" title="Bismuth(III) iodide"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></a>. All of these except <span class="chemf nowrap">BiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> are <a href="/wiki/Hydrolyze" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrolyze">hydrolyzed</a> by water.<sup id="cite_ref-norman1_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norman1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth(III) chloride">Bismuth(III) chloride</a> reacts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a> solution to produce the acid <span class="chemf nowrap">HBiCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-s8_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The oxidation state +5 is less frequently encountered. One such compound is <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_pentafluoride" title="Bismuth pentafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">BiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span></a>, a powerful oxidizing and fluorinating agent. It is also a strong fluoride acceptor, reacting with <a href="/wiki/Xenon_tetrafluoride" title="Xenon tetrafluoride">xenon tetrafluoride</a> to form the <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> cation:<sup id="cite_ref-s8_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s8-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">BiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>BiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aqueous_species">Aqueous species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Aqueous species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Aqueous" class="mw-redirect" title="Aqueous">aqueous</a> solution, the Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span> ion is solvated to form the aqua ion <span class="chemf nowrap">Bi(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span> in strongly acidic conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Persson2010_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Persson2010-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> At pH &gt; 0 polynuclear species exist, the most important of which is believed to be the octahedral complex [<span class="chemf nowrap">Bi<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-NäslundPersson2000_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NäslundPersson2000-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_bismuth_production" title="List of countries by bismuth production">List of countries by bismuth production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Bismite.jpg/170px-Bismite.jpg" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Bismite.jpg/255px-Bismite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Bismite.jpg/340px-Bismite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bismite" title="Bismite">Bismite</a> mineral</div></div></div>
<p>In the Earth's crust, bismuth is about <a href="/wiki/Abundances_of_the_elements_(data_page)" title="Abundances of the elements (data page)">twice as abundant as gold</a>. The most important <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a> of bismuth are <a href="/wiki/Bismuthinite" title="Bismuthinite">bismuthinite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismite" title="Bismite">bismite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Native bismuth is known from Australia, Bolivia, and China.<sup id="cite_ref-arizona1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arizona1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a>, the world mining production of bismuth in 2014 was 13,600 tonnes, with the major contributions from China (7,600 tonnes), Vietnam (4,950 tonnes) and Mexico (948 tonnes).<sup id="cite_ref-usgs2016_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2016-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The refinery production in 2010 was 16,000 tonnes, of which China produced 13,000, Mexico 850 and Belgium 800 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs2010_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2010-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The difference reflects bismuth's status as a byproduct of extraction of other metals such as lead, copper, tin, molybdenum and tungsten.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> World bismuth production from refineries is a more complete and reliable statistic.<sup id="cite_ref-Oje_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oje-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth travels in crude lead bullion (which can contain up to 10% bismuth) through several stages of refining, until it is removed by the <a href="/wiki/Kroll-Betterton_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Kroll-Betterton process">Kroll-Betterton process</a> which separates the impurities as slag, or the electrolytic <a href="/wiki/Betts_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Betts process">Betts process</a>. Bismuth will behave similarly with another of its major metals, copper.<sup id="cite_ref-Oje_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oje-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The raw bismuth metal from both processes contains still considerable amounts of other metals, foremost lead. By reacting the molten mixture with chlorine gas the metals are converted to their chlorides while bismuth remains unchanged. Impurities can also be removed by various other methods for example with fluxes and treatments yielding high-purity bismuth metal (over 99% Bi).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Price">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BiPrice.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/BiPrice.png/290px-BiPrice.png" width="290" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/BiPrice.png/435px-BiPrice.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/BiPrice.png/580px-BiPrice.png 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="940" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BiPrice.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World mine production and annual averages of bismuth price (New York, not adjusted for inflation).<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The price for pure bismuth metal has been relatively stable through most of the 20th century, except for a spike in the 1970s. Bismuth has always been produced mainly as a byproduct of lead refining, and thus the price usually reflected the cost of recovery and the balance between production and demand.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Demand for bismuth was small prior to World War II and was pharmaceutical – bismuth compounds were used to treat such conditions as digestive disorders, <a href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infection" title="Sexually transmitted infection">sexually transmitted infections</a> and burns. Minor amounts of bismuth metal were consumed in fusible alloys for <a href="/wiki/Fire_sprinkler" title="Fire sprinkler">fire sprinkler</a> systems and <a href="/wiki/Fuse_(electrical)" title="Fuse (electrical)">fuse wire</a>. During World War II bismuth was considered a <a href="/wiki/Strategic_material" title="Strategic material">strategic material</a>, used for solders, fusible alloys, medications and atomic research. To stabilize the market, the producers set the price at $1.25 per pound (2.75 $/kg) during the war and at $2.25 per pound (4.96 $/kg) from 1950 until 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1970s, the price rose rapidly as a result of increasing demand for bismuth as a metallurgical additive to aluminium, iron and steel. This was followed by a decline owing to increased world production, stabilized consumption, and the recessions of 1980 and 1981–82. In 1984, the price began to climb as consumption increased worldwide, especially in the United States and Japan. In the early 1990s, research began on the evaluation of bismuth as a nontoxic replacement for lead in ceramic glazes, fishing sinkers, food-processing equipment, free-machining <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brasses</a> for plumbing applications, lubricating greases, and shot for <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl_hunting" title="Waterfowl hunting">waterfowl hunting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-s14_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s14-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Growth in these areas remained slow during the middle 1990s, in spite of the backing of lead replacement by the US Government, but intensified around 2005. This resulted in a rapid and continuing increase in price.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most bismuth is produced as a byproduct of other metal-extraction processes including the smelting of lead, and also of tungsten and copper. Its <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> is dependent on increased recycling, which is problematic.
</p><p>It was once believed that bismuth could be practically recycled from the soldered joints in electronic equipment. Recent efficiencies in solder application in electronics mean there is substantially less solder deposited, and thus less to recycle. While recovering the silver from silver-bearing solder may remain economic, recovering bismuth is substantially less so.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Next in recycling feasibility would be sizeable catalysts with a fair bismuth content, such as bismuth phosphomolybdate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, bismuth used in galvanizing, and as a free-machining metallurgical additive.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Bismuth in uses where it is dispersed most widely include certain stomach medicines (<a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">bismuth subsalicylate</a>), paints (<a href="/wiki/Bismuth_vanadate" title="Bismuth vanadate">bismuth vanadate</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pearlescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pearlescent">pearlescent</a> cosmetics (<a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxychloride" title="Bismuth oxychloride">bismuth oxychloride</a>), and bismuth-containing bullets. Recycling bismuth from these uses is impractical.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Bismuth has few commercial applications, and those applications that use it generally require small quantities relative to other raw materials. In the United States, for example, 884 tonnes of bismuth were consumed in 2010, of which 63% went into chemicals (including pharmaceuticals, pigments, and cosmetics); 26% into metallurgical additives for casting and galvanizing;<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> 7% into bismuth alloys, solders and ammunition; and 4% into research and other uses.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs2010_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2010-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some manufacturers use bismuth as a substitute in equipment for potable water systems such as valves to meet "lead-free" mandates in the U.S. (began in 2014).  This is a fairly large application since it covers all residential and commercial building construction.
</p><p>In the early 1990s, researchers began to evaluate bismuth as a nontoxic replacement for lead in various applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicines">Medicines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Medicines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bismuth is an ingredient in some pharmaceuticals,<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> although the use of some of these substances is declining.<sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">Bismuth subsalicylate</a> is used as an <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">antidiarrheal</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> it is the <a href="/wiki/Active_ingredient" title="Active ingredient">active ingredient</a> in such "Pink Bismuth" preparations as <a href="/wiki/Pepto-Bismol" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepto-Bismol">Pepto-Bismol</a>, as well as the 2004 reformulation of <a href="/wiki/Kaopectate" title="Kaopectate">Kaopectate</a>. It is also used to treat some other gastro-intestinal diseases and <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_poisoning" title="Cadmium poisoning">cadmium poisoning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The mechanism of action of this substance is still not well documented, although an <a href="/wiki/Oligodynamic_effect" title="Oligodynamic effect">oligodynamic effect</a> (toxic effect of small doses of heavy metal ions on microbes) may be involved in at least some cases. <a href="/wiki/Salicylic_acid" title="Salicylic acid">Salicylic acid</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of the compound is antimicrobial for toxogenic <i>E. coli,</i> an important pathogen in <a href="/wiki/Traveler%27s_diarrhea" title="Traveler&#39;s diarrhea">traveler's diarrhea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>a combination of <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">bismuth subsalicylate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subcitrate" title="Bismuth subcitrate">bismuth subcitrate</a> is used to treat the bacteria causing <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" class="mw-redirect" title="Peptic ulcer">peptic ulcers</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bibrocathol" title="Bibrocathol">Bibrocathol</a> is an organic bismuth-containing compound used to treat eye infections.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subgallate" title="Bismuth subgallate">Bismuth subgallate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Active_ingredient" title="Active ingredient">active ingredient</a> in Devrom, is used as an internal deodorant to treat malodor from <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>.</li>
<li>Bismuth compounds (including <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sodium_bismuth_tartrate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sodium bismuth tartrate (page does not exist)">sodium bismuth tartrate</a>) were formerly used to treat syphilis<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>"Milk of bismuth" (an aqueous solution of <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_hydroxide" title="Bismuth hydroxide">bismuth hydroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subcarbonate" title="Bismuth subcarbonate">bismuth subcarbonate</a>) was marketed as an alimentary cure-all in the early 20th century</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subnitrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth subnitrate">Bismuth subnitrate</a> (Bi<sub>5</sub>O(OH)<sub>9</sub>(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subcarbonate" title="Bismuth subcarbonate">bismuth subcarbonate</a> (Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(CO<sub>3</sub>)) are also used in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmetics_and_pigments">Cosmetics and pigments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cosmetics and pigments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxychloride" title="Bismuth oxychloride">Bismuth oxychloride</a> (BiOCl) is sometimes used in cosmetics, as a pigment in paint for eye shadows, hair sprays and nail polishes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Effp_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effp-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Paff_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paff-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> This compound is found as the mineral bismoclite and in crystal form contains layers of atoms (see figure above) that refract light chromatically, resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Iridescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridescent">iridescent</a> appearance similar to <a href="/wiki/Nacre" title="Nacre">nacre</a> of pearl. It was used as a cosmetic in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> and in many places since. <i>Bismuth white</i> (also "Spanish white") can refer to either bismuth oxychloride or <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxynitrate" title="Bismuth oxynitrate">bismuth oxynitrate</a> (BiONO<sub>3</sub>), when used as a white pigment. Bismuth vanadate is used as a light-stable non-reactive paint pigment (particularly for artists' paints), often as a replacement for the more toxic cadmium sulfide yellow and orange-yellow pigments. The most common variety in artists' paints is a lemon yellow, visually indistinguishable from its cadmium-containing alternative.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_and_alloys">Metal and alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Metal and alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bismuth is used in metal alloys with other metals such as iron, to create alloys to go into automatic sprinkler systems for fires. It was also used to make <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_bronze" title="Bismuth bronze">bismuth bronze</a> which was used in the Bronze Age.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lead_replacement">Lead replacement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Lead replacement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The density difference between lead (11.32&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>) and bismuth (9.78&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>) is small enough that for many <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">ballistics</a> and weighting applications, bismuth can substitute for <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>. For example, it can replace lead as a dense material in <a href="/wiki/Fishing_sinker" title="Fishing sinker">fishing sinkers</a>. It has been used as a replacement for lead in <a href="/wiki/Shot_(pellet)" title="Shot (pellet)">shot</a>, bullets and <a href="/wiki/Less-lethal" class="mw-redirect" title="Less-lethal">less-lethal</a> <a href="/wiki/Riot_gun" title="Riot gun">riot gun</a> ammunition. The Netherlands, Denmark, England,  Wales, the US, and many other countries now prohibit the use of lead shot for the hunting of wetland birds, as many birds are prone to <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a> owing to mistaken ingestion of lead (instead of small stones and grit) to aid digestion, or even prohibit the use of lead for all hunting, such as in the Netherlands. Bismuth-tin alloy shot is one alternative that provides similar ballistic performance to lead. (Another less expensive but also more poorly performing alternative is "steel" shot, which is actually soft iron.) Bismuth's lack of <a href="/wiki/Malleability" class="mw-redirect" title="Malleability">malleability</a> does, however, make it unsuitable for use in expanding hunting bullets.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole paragraph (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Bismuth, as a dense element of high atomic weight, is used in bismuth-impregnated <a href="/w/index.php?title=Latex_shield&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Latex shield (page does not exist)">latex shields</a> to shield from X-ray in medical examinations, such as <a href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" class="mw-redirect" title="X-ray computed tomography">CTs</a>, mostly as it is considered non-toxic.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Restriction_of_Hazardous_Substances_Directive" title="Restriction of Hazardous Substances Directive">Restriction of Hazardous Substances Directive</a> (RoHS) for reduction of lead has broadened bismuth's use in electronics as a component of low-melting point solders, as a replacement for traditional tin-lead solders.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs2010_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2010-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Its low toxicity will be especially important for solders to be used in food processing equipment and copper water pipes, although it can also be used in other applications including those in the automobile industry, in the EU for example.<sup id="cite_ref-lohse_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lohse-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth has been evaluated as a replacement for lead in free-machining <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brasses</a> for <a href="/wiki/Plumbing" title="Plumbing">plumbing</a> applications,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> although it does not equal the performance of leaded steels.<sup id="cite_ref-lohse_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lohse-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
