<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_metal_uses_and_specialty_alloys">Other metal uses and specialty alloys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Other metal uses and specialty alloys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many bismuth <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> have low <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting points</a> and are found in specialty applications such as <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solders</a>. Many automatic sprinklers, electric fuses, and safety devices in fire detection and suppression systems contain the eutectic In19.1-Cd5.3-Pb22.6-Sn8.3-Bi44.7 alloy that melts at 47&#160;°C (117&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  This is a convenient temperature since it is unlikely to be exceeded in normal living conditions. Low-melting alloys, such as Bi-Cd-Pb-Sn alloy which melts at 70&#160;°C, are also used in automotive and aviation industries. Before deforming a thin-walled metal part, it is filled with a melt or covered with a thin layer of the alloy to reduce the chance of breaking. Then the alloy is removed by submerging the part in boiling water.<sup id="cite_ref-k183_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k183-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth is used to make <a href="/wiki/Free-machining_steel" class="mw-redirect" title="Free-machining steel">free-machining steels</a> and free-machining aluminium alloys for precision machining properties. It has similar effect to lead and improves the chip breaking during machining. The shrinking on solidification in lead and the expansion of bismuth compensate each other and therefore lead and bismuth are often used in similar quantities.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, alloys containing comparable parts of bismuth and lead exhibit a very small change (on the order 0.01%) upon melting, solidification or aging. Such alloys are used in high-precision casting, e.g. in dentistry, to create models and molds.<sup id="cite_ref-k183_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k183-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Bismuth is also used as an alloying agent in production of malleable irons and as a <a href="/wiki/Thermocouple" title="Thermocouple">thermocouple</a> material.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usgs2010_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs2010-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth is also used in aluminium-silicon cast alloys in order to refine silicon morphology. However, it indicated a poisoning effect on modification of strontium (Sr).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Some bismuth alloys, such as Bi35-Pb37-Sn25, are combined with non-sticking materials such as <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a>, glass and <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_enamel" title="Vitreous enamel">enamels</a> because they easily wet them allowing to make joints to other parts. Addition of bismuth to caesium enhances the quantum yield of caesium cathodes.<sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">Sintering</a> of bismuth and manganese powders at 300&#160;°C produces a permanent magnet and <a href="/wiki/Magnetostrictive" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetostrictive">magnetostrictive</a> material, which is used in ultrasonic generators and receivers working in the 10–100&#160;kHz range and in magnetic memory devices.<sup id="cite_ref-s15_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s15-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses_as_compounds">Other uses as compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other uses as compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuthvanadat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bismuthvanadat.jpg/220px-Bismuthvanadat.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Bismuthvanadat.jpg/330px-Bismuthvanadat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Bismuthvanadat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="393" data-file-height="322" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bismuthvanadat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bismuth vanadate, a yellow pigment</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Bismuth is included in <a href="/wiki/BSCCO" class="mw-redirect" title="BSCCO">BSCCO</a> (bismuth strontium calcium copper oxide) which is a group of similar superconducting compounds discovered in 1988 that exhibit the highest superconducting transition temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subnitrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth subnitrate">Bismuth subnitrate</a> is a component of <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glazes</a> that produces an <a href="/wiki/Iridescence" title="Iridescence">iridescence</a> and is used as a pigment in paint.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_telluride" title="Bismuth telluride">Bismuth telluride</a> is a semiconductor and an excellent <a href="/wiki/Thermoelectric_effect" title="Thermoelectric effect">thermoelectric</a> material.<sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Bi<sub>2</sub>Te<sub>3</sub> diodes are used in mobile refrigerators, <a href="/wiki/CPU" class="mw-redirect" title="CPU">CPU</a> coolers, and as detectors in <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> spectrophotometers.<sup id="cite_ref-k184_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k184-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_oxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismuth oxide">Bismuth oxide</a>, in its delta form, is a solid electrolyte for oxygen. This form normally breaks down below a high-temperature threshold, but can be electrodeposited well below this temperature in a highly alkaline solution.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_vanadate" title="Bismuth vanadate">Bismuth vanadate</a> is an opaque yellow pigment used by some artists' oil, acrylic, and watercolor paint companies, primarily as a replacement for the more toxic cadmium sulfide yellows in the greenish-yellow (lemon) to orange-toned yellow range. It performs practically identically to the cadmium pigments, such as in terms of resistance to degradation from UV exposure, opacity, tinting strength, and lack of reactivity when mixed with other pigments. The most commonly-used variety by artists' paint makers is lemon in color. In addition to being a replacement for several cadmium yellows, it also serves as a non-toxic visual replacement for the older chromate pigments made with zinc, lead, and strontium. If a green pigment and barium sulfate (for increased transparency) are added it can also serve as a replacement for barium chromate, which possesses a more greenish cast than the others. In comparison with lead chromates, it does not blacken due to hydrogen sulfide in the air (a process accelerated by UV exposure) and possesses a particularly brighter color than them, especially the lemon, which is the most translucent, dull, and fastest to blacken due to the higher percentage of lead sulfate required to produce that shade. It is also used, on a limited basis due to its cost, as an vehicle paint pigment.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>A catalyst for making acrylic fibers.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>As an <a href="/wiki/Electrocatalyst" title="Electrocatalyst">electrocatalyst</a> in the conversion of CO<sub>2</sub> to CO.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Lubrication" title="Lubrication">lubricating</a> <a href="/wiki/Grease_(lubricant)" title="Grease (lubricant)">greases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In crackling microstars (<a href="/wiki/Dragon%27s_eggs" class="mw-redirect" title="Dragon&#39;s eggs">dragon's eggs</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/Bismuth(III)_oxide" title="Bismuth(III) oxide">oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subcarbonate" title="Bismuth subcarbonate">subcarbonate</a> or subnitrate.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicology_and_ecotoxicology">Toxicology and ecotoxicology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Toxicology and ecotoxicology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl><dd><i>See also <a href="/wiki/Bismuthia" title="Bismuthia">bismuthia</a>, a rare dermatological condition that results from the prolonged use of bismuth.</i></dd></dl>
<p>Scientific literature indicates that some of the compounds of bismuth are less toxic to humans via ingestion compared to other heavy metals (lead, arsenic, antimony, etc.)<sup id="cite_ref-Kean_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> presumably due to the comparatively low solubility of bismuth salts.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>  Its biological half-life for whole-body retention is reported to be 5 days but it can remain in the kidney for years in people treated with bismuth compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Fowler_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth poisoning can occur and has according to some reports been common in relatively recent times.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lenntech_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenntech-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> As with lead, bismuth poisoning can result in the formation of a black deposit on the <a href="/wiki/Gingiva" class="mw-redirect" title="Gingiva">gingiva</a>, known as a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth_line&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bismuth line (page does not exist)">bismuth line</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-biline_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biline-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Poisoning may be treated with <a href="/wiki/Dimercaprol" title="Dimercaprol">dimercaprol</a>; however, evidence for benefit is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2008_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2008-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AHFS2016_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AHFS2016-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bismuth's environmental impacts are not well known; it may be less likely to bioaccumulate than some other heavy metals, and this is an area of active research.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioremediation">Bioremediation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bismuth&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Bioremediation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Marasmius_oreades" title="Marasmius oreades">Marasmius oreades</a></i> can be used for the <a href="/wiki/Biological_remediation" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological remediation">biological remediation</a> of bismuth in polluted soils.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuthine" title="Bismuthine">BiH<sub>3</sub></a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">BiOHOCOC<sub>6</sub>H<sub>4</sub>O</a></li></ul></div><table class="nowraplinks navbox-subgroup" style="border-spacing:0"><tbody><tr><th id="Organobismuth(III)" scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%">Organobismuth(III)</th><td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<li><a href="/wiki/Bismole" title="Bismole">C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>4</sub>BiH</a></li>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table><div>
</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%">Bismuth(V)</th><td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
